Often specialized solutions exist, for example:\n   178→\n   179→    - `scipy.signal.fftconvolve`\n   180→\n   181→    - filtering functions in `scipy.ndimage`\n   182→\n   183→    - moving window functions provided by\n   184→      `bottleneck <https://github.com/pydata/bottleneck>`_.\n   185→\n   186→    As a rough estimate, a sliding window approach with an input size of `N`\n   187→    and a window size of `W` will scale as `O(N*W)` where frequently a special\n   188→    algorithm can achieve `O(N)`. Single integers `i` are treated as if they were the\n        tuple `(i,)`.\n    axis : int or tuple of int, optional\n        Axis or axes along which the sliding window is applied.\n        By default, the sliding window is applied to all axes and\n        `window_shape[i]` will refer to axis `i` of `x`.\n        If `axis` is given as a `tuple of int`, `window_shape[i]` will refer to\n        the axis `axis[i]` of `x`.\n        Single integers `i` are treated as if they were the tuple `(i,)`.\n    subok : bool, optional\n        If True, sub-classes will be passed-through, otherwise the returned\n        array will be forced to be a base-class array (default).\n    writeable : bool, optional\n        When true, allow writing to the returned view. The default is false,\n        as this should be used with caution: the returned view contains the\n        same memory location multiple times, so writing to one location will\n        cause others to change.\n\n    Returns\n    -------\n    view : ndarray\n        Sliding window view of the array. The sliding window dimensions are\n        inserted at the end, and the original dimensions are trimmed as\n        required by the size of the sliding window.\n        That is, ``view.shape = x_shape_trimmed + window_shape``, where\n        ``x_shape_trimmed`` is ``x.shape`` with every entry reduced by one less\n        than the corresponding window size.\n\n    See Also\n    --------\n    lib.stride_tricks.as_strided: A lower-level and less safe routine for\n        creating arbitrary views from custom shape and strides.\n    broadcast_to: broadcast an array to a given shape.\n\n    Notes\n    -----\n    For many applications using a sliding window view can be convenient, but\n    potentially very slow. Often specialized solutions exist, for example:\n\n    - `scipy.signal.fftconvolve`\n\n    - filtering functions in `scipy.ndimage`\n\n    - moving window functions provided by\n      `bottleneck <https://github.com/pydata/bottleneck>`_.\n\n    As a rough estimate, a sliding window approach with an input size of `N`\n    and a window size of `W` will scale as `O(N*W)` where frequently a special\n    algorithm can achieve `O(N)`. That means that the sliding window variant\n    for a window size of 100 can be a 100 times slower than a more specialized\n    version.\n\n    Nevertheless, for small window sizes, when no custom algorithm exists, or\n    as a prototyping and developing tool, this function can be a good solution.\n\n    Examples\n    --------\n    >>> import numpy as np\n    >>> from numpy.lib.stride_tricks import sliding_window_view\n    >>> x = np.arange(6)\n    >>> x.shape\n    (6,)\n    >>> v = sliding_window_view(x, 3)\n    >>> v.shape\n    (4, 3)\n    >>> v\n    array([[0, 1, 2],\n           [1, 2, 3],\n           [2, 3, 4],\n           [3, 4, 5]])\n\n    This also works in more dimensions, e.g.\n\n    >>> i, j = np.ogrid[:3, :4]\n    >>> x = 10*i + j\n    >>> x.shape\n    (3, 4)\n    >>> x\n    array([[ 0,  1,  2,  3],\n           [10, 11, 12, 13],\n           [20, 21, 22, 23]])\n    >>> shape = (2,2)\n    >>> v = sliding_window_view(x, shape)\n    >>> v.shape\n    (2, 3, 2, 2)\n    >>> v\n    array([[[[ 0,  1],\n             [10, 11]],\n            [[ 1,  2],\n             [11, 12]],\n            [[ 2,  3],\n             [12, 13]]],\n           [[[10, 11],\n             [20, 21]],\n            [[11, 12],\n             [21, 22]],\n            [[12, 13],\n             [22, 23]]]])\n\n    The axis can be specified explicitly:\n\n    >>> v = sliding_window_view(x, 3, 0)\n    >>> v.shape\n    (1, 4, 3)\n    >>> v\n    array([[[ 0, 10, 20],\n            [ 1, 11, 21],\n            [ 2, 12, 22],\n            [ 3, 13, 23]]])\n\n    The same axis can be used several times. Default is -1 (last axis).\n    mode : {'valid', 'same', 'full'}, optional\n        Mode for handling boundaries:\n\n        - 'valid': only compute median where window is completely within array\n        - 'same': pad array so output has same shape as input\n        - 'full': pad array so window can start/end at array boundaries\n\n        Default is 'valid'.\n\n    Returns\n    -------\n    rolling_median : ndarray\n        Array of rolling medians with the same dtype as input (or float64 for\n        integer inputs smaller than float64). Shape depends on the mode parameter.\n\n    See Also\n    --------\n    median : Compute the median of array elements\n    numpy.lib.stride_tricks.sliding_window_view : Create a sliding window view\n\n    Notes\n    -----\n    The rolling median is computed using sliding windows. For 'valid' mode,\n    the output length along the specified axis will be ``len(a) - window_size + 1``.\n\n    Examples\n    --------\n    >>> import numpy as np\n    >>> a = np.array([1, 2, 3, 4, 5])\n    >>> np.rolling_median(a, 3)\n    array([2., 3., 4.])\n\n    Using the 'same' mode to get output with the same shape as input:\n\n    >>> np.rolling_median(a, 3, mode='same')\n    array([1.5, 2. , 3. , 4. , 4.5])\n    \"\"\"\n    a = np.asanyarray(a)\n\n    if not isinstance(window_size, int) or window_size < 1:\n        raise ValueError(\"window_size must be a positive integer\")\n\n    if mode not in ('valid', 'same', 'full'):\n        raise ValueError(\"mode must be 'valid', 'same', or 'full'\")\n\n    # Normalize axis\n    if axis is None:\n        axis = -1\n    axis = normalize_axis_index(axis, a.ndim)\n\n    # Get the length along the axis\n    axis_len = a.shape[axis]\n\n    # Handle edge case where window_size is larger than array\n    if window_size > axis_len:\n        if mode == 'valid':\n            # For 'valid' mode with window larger than array, return empty\n            new_shape = list(a.shape)\n            new_shape[axis] = 0\n            return np.array([], dtype=np.float64 if np.issubdtype(a.dtype, np.integer) else a.dtype).reshape(new_shape)\n        elif mode == 'same':\n            # For 'same' mode, return median along entire axis\n            return np.median(a, axis=axis, keepdims=True)\n        else:  # mode == 'full'\n            # For 'full' mode, need to handle carefully\n            pass\n\n    # Move axis to the last position for easier processing\n    a_moved = np.moveaxis(a, axis, -1)\n    original_shape = a_moved.shape\n\n    # Reshape to 2D for simpler processing\n    a_2d = a_moved.reshape(-1, original_shape[-1])\n\n    # Compute rolling median for each row\n    if mode == 'valid':\n        output_len = a_2d.shape[-1] - window_size + 1\n        result = np.empty((a_2d.shape[0], output_len), dtype=np.float64 if np.issubdtype(a.dtype, np.integer) else a.dtype)\n\n        for i in range(output_len):\n            window = a_2d[:, i:i+window_size]\n            result[:, i] = np.median(window, axis=1)\n\n    elif mode == 'same':\n        result = np.empty_like(a_2d, dtype=np.float64 if np.issubdtype(a.dtype, np.integer) else a.dtype)\n\n        # Compute rolling median with padding\n        offset = window_size // 2\n\n        for i in range(a_2d.shape[-1]):\n            start = max(0, i - offset)\n            end = min(a_2d.shape[-1], i + window_size - offset)\n            window = a_2d[:, start:end]\n            result[:, i] = np.median(window, axis=1)\n\n    else:  # mode == 'full'\n        output_len = a_2d.shape[-1] + window_size - 1\n        result = np.empty((a_2d.shape[0], output_len), dtype=np.float64 if np.issubdtype(a.dtype, np.integer) else a.dtype)\n\n        # Compute rolling median with full padding\n        for i in range(output_len):\n            start = max(0, i - window_size + 1)\n            end = min(a_2d.shape[-1], i + 1)\n            window = a_2d[:, start:end]\n            result[:, i] = np.median(window, axis=1)\n\n    # Reshape back to original shape (with modified axis length)\n    if mode == 'valid':\n        output_shape = list(original_shape)\n        output_shape[-1] = a_2d.shape[-1] - The key difference is how the window is positioned: 'valid' only computes where the window fully fits, 'same' pads conceptually to maintain input size, and 'full' includes all partial window positions at the boundaries.\n\nWhen the window reaches the edges, I need to decide whether to compute the median from however many elements are available or to use padding. Computing from available elements seems more practical than introducing NaN values or sentinels.","signature":"EqgaCkYICxgCKkAF9ExJ/jKwbdJlKH6fVXgG3tPCDVEezZ75G9NEVLb6ZS9QeDzEhonvRm0FMkI27CppoVLLdyxrSPGVqIfLPderEgwLOgsL4T3Utkd6LMwaDDueJUHNUe3a3vzlqiIwYwQwCskB+qP/xFrvXfFPEym6Dfi++2CiKPgyyIpE9NZsspwlkqQeLbCAK+uq5fZtKo8Z3Le10tJltAf2KKRrQmROtJVQU5/Pqbx1gVCtVmLxZOeCmxmCGZGY+0OLOWoT2+yExK4HiPcoR5rCI3hvd283VVQW+d6CT4dvW/fuFbbt1CKu6PhjfA+KC4gvutt90eb+muucOOf0xTNH83APTBYt89/dfUq4zyMFKCjvk4yC/u9Uk68PquC5QnrXrrJhDYHCUTjk2iyHILLd1kVImKkuuk6XBBOUsnqEszajVXiAWVAF7jMgSWtTnMnjfgvhPpItIP0FWOd9ONcg+3l7tXbGt+SJlVbJdrO3pEzHkvPquX8hCxTFxSFyQUytQMxiWr2BeJ06BFSn8aoG20SdbbqDvs8QHkC6nyceqzUTzm3ETGC/1Eta0R+s1JdwiSNx3jOMwbX4wx7JiQRoIbJWz3h0Ljm1PVQxHJCmr4xdqbDTUn7E+ZJjexSPmh6jdD4GjvjX+Cq7bEzMotVsqSJ24trI+K2OiP36fDS6mQPqJmuLOjLkxOk4Ps5hznsiL85LvSl9Ec6JT9Tagr5Mf4ePnqNZz9sEQnoySCiJcMGUSuy4fHTNvLCitd2NHZPMWZm3wC9ivMGtuLVUyVpm2gazz/qX21T/Ll1tMSm4xvjtoKytA5amLzHeGKnNu0b1Kfv+phxPODawUW82dt7OBUeeudWX8H8CTE/cYwMWIbyB+FXbjf/brpMrwfLHBm8G+bciQQuHb4Ujq9h3GagBMWck2APris/6tBf0oahN+JFc+9IukT6hFCeGypNNqF0xpevPVZgWyiYdYLQKF9sKhRy1YwqEl85iDa76piHXLewfCM2c/49SRrY6G/5LmlstWBxtFgdGsmx+8oYKWe5pthl6nUanXcuFcUFs1v5IvQ61bLV/gzl6DHrIy3gK7wuyZ/CcdHjg1sJSZ2CGZsT+HOwnJ5WSoUaofxE1jkX21r4C9lq6PiAGuKeY/XiU9YsirAKS0fmjw91mof8I7gqwqA4yeUgRE3LSR/RmxaSZ1igNVAURRSMVg+fq2YeEJOX61FoZXBB+SD5wuogMiU5y2mSIILFDb24kh4dOx4rnj6OlTb8IMTW59x6/iScp2t6b63aFhD74EAykJEymy36wpiiTbKj0mOOdMOu7brPKV5e9tIIPC36FgEGojSCHsAxyX6tgQTcEwby6ejhpbL+1O0FBn3E/6mO7zr1IIRPrtsUSymHGl5QOHIVQR9CG5Pge8yg7clQ0hfcZgXrIZN9DpX7SIArlOu8le/M5xCkFoazkLCMBvBegGnof17vQtLt9aPPDgzYa9KroduLMWJS4jAnVdolD53iZwJHAUFHRrVj3wesr1peyXy/YEKY+PS1UUgmq3HHxWsv6l/cRmYBGWzBl4ZLMSipY2ilYpPEt+iuKSHKFnDioMltk9cAidBEP8JJaYLdZsYWQY8mdqOUjIYhEgOssthPRzqCKB4f/bboMoItYiJWZ9xBMn7tmHybiuUaX1nLj1/1+lAt7UjWzSdzGS2PSk3BUzT+c+rGYOl0nRWa+/N9ExL6L/gWz9MrI8WaNmAjDOiup/H24v7bouQe2SASulB1P0eNdJB9gdS+IeFFcBZJ4w8rbOz8EfvVyX7yLIyz+xRoiASQ4IeKH0WWhPNfWWqQg5EzjmMwmWEu0uOb7uEdOsR/tznNv3GW26bfrznFO7tyq3pOUSC5bgDo2zrqvbUmfn+PW+fmbGaTjHQE7MNS6VSTDAJ4vvIPqtfMXnd9HUVP65aAdVrOCE4h07NWwI8tQ/oXnOa8SIbtiKYX For 'valid' mode,\n    the output length along the specified axis will be ``len(a) - window_size + 1``.\n\n    Examples\n    --------\n    >>> import numpy as np\n    >>> a = np.array([1, 2, 3, 4, 5])\n    >>> np.rolling_median(a, 3)\n    array([2., 3., 4.])\n\n    Using the 'same' mode to get output with the same shape as input:\n\n    >>> np.rolling_median(a, 3, mode='same')\n    array([1.5, 2. , 3. , 4. , 4.5])\n    \"\"\"\n    a = np.asanyarray(a)\n\n    if not isinstance(window_size, int) or window_size < 1:\n        raise ValueError(\"window_size must be a positive integer\")\n\n    if mode not in ('valid', 'same', 'full'):\n        raise ValueError(\"mode must be 'valid', 'same', or 'full'\")\n\n    # Normalize axis\n    if axis is None:\n        axis = -1\n    axis = normalize_axis_index(axis, a.ndim)\n\n    # Get the length along the axis\n    axis_len = a.shape[axis]\n\n    # Handle edge case where window_size is larger than array\n    if window_size > axis_len:\n        if mode == 'valid':\n            # For 'valid' mode with window larger than array, return empty\n            new_shape = list(a.shape)\n            new_shape[axis] = 0\n            dtype = np.float64 if np.issubdtype(a.dtype, np.integer) else a.dtype\n            empty_array = np.array([], dtype=dtype)\n            return empty_array.reshape(new_shape)\n        elif mode == 'same':\n            # For 'same' mode, compute median of available elements for each position\n            # This will be handled in the main loop\n            pass\n        else:  # mode == 'full'\n            # For 'full' mode, will be handled in the main loop\n            pass\n\n    # Move axis to the last position for easier processing\n    a_moved = np.moveaxis(a, axis, -1)\n    original_shape = a_moved.shape\n\n    # Reshape to 2D for simpler processing\n    a_2d = a_moved.reshape(-1, original_shape[-1])\n\n    # Compute rolling median for each row\n    if mode == 'valid':\n        output_len = a_2d.shape[-1] - offset)\n            end = min(a_2d.shape[-1], i + window_size - offset)\n            window = a_2d[:, start:end]\n            result[:, i] = np.median(window, axis=1)\n\n    else:  # mode == 'full'\n        output_len = a_2d.shape[-1] + window_size - 1\n        result = np.empty((a_2d.shape[0], output_len), dtype=np.float64 if np.issubdtype(a.dtype, np.integer) else a.dtype)\n\n        # Compute rolling median with full padding\n        for i in range(output_len):\n            start = max(0, i - Default is -1 (last axis).\n    mode : {'valid', 'same', 'full'}, optional\n        Mode for handling boundaries:\n\n        - 'valid': only compute median where window is completely within array\n        - 'same': pad array so output has same shape as input\n        - 'full': pad array so window can start/end at array boundaries\n\n        Default is 'valid'.\n\n    Returns\n    -------\n    rolling_median : ndarray\n        Array of rolling medians with the same dtype as input (or float64 for\n        integer inputs smaller than float64). Shape depends on the mode parameter.\n\n    See Also\n    --------\n    median : Compute the median of array elements\n    numpy.lib.stride_tricks.sliding_window_view : Create a sliding window view\n\n    Notes\n    -----\n    The rolling median is computed using sliding windows. For 'valid' mode,\n    the output length along the specified axis will be ``len(a) - window_size + 1``.\n\n    Examples\n    --------\n    >>> import numpy as np\n    >>> a = np.array([1, 2, 3, 4, 5])\n    >>> np.rolling_median(a, 3)\n    array([2., 3., 4.])\n\n    Using the 'same' mode to get output with the same shape as input:\n\n    >>> np.rolling_median(a, 3, mode='same')\n    array([1.5, 2. , 3. , 4. , 4.5])\n    \"\"\"\n    a = np.asanyarray(a)\n\n    if not isinstance(window_size, int) or window_size < 1:\n        raise ValueError(\"window_size must be a positive integer\")\n\n    if mode not in ('valid', 'same', 'full'):\n        raise ValueError(\"mode must be 'valid', 'same', or 'full'\")\n\n    # Normalize axis\n    if axis is None:\n        axis = -1\n    axis = normalize_axis_index(axis, a.ndim)\n\n    # Get the length along the axis\n    axis_len = a.shape[axis]\n\n    # Handle edge case where window_size is larger than array\n    if window_size > axis_len:\n        if mode == 'valid':\n            # For 'same' mode, compute median of available elements for each position\n            # This will be handled in the main loop\n            pass\n        else:  # mode == 'full'\n            # For 'full' mode, will be handled in the main loop\n            pass\n\n    # Determine output dtype by computing median of a small window\n    if a_2d.shape[-1] >= window_size:\n        sample_window = a_2d[0, :window_size]\n    else:\n        sample_window = a_2d[0, :]\n    sample_dtype = np.median(sample_window).dtype\n\n    #
